From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Sep  2 06:03:54 2008
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 2 Sep 2008 06:03:54 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Postfix Linux-only local denial of service (fwd)
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0809020602390.16727@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   FYI

Rich

-- 
Richard B. Shepard, Ph.D.               |  Integrity            Credibility
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc.        |            Innovation
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517      Fax: 503-667-8863

---------- Forwarded message ----------
Date: Tue, 2 Sep 2008 08:52:44 -0400 (EDT)
From: Wietse Venema <wietse at porcupine.org>

An on-line version of this announcement is available at
http://www.postfix.org/announcements/20080902.html

Summary:
========
Postfix 2.4 and later, on Linux kernel 2.6, is vulnerable to a denial of
service attack by a local user. There is no breach of data confidentiality
or data integrity. This problem was found by the Postfix author during
routine source code maintenance.

Discussion:
===========
Postfix is an open-source mail transfer agent (MTA) that runs on multiple
types of UNIX systems.  Postfix 2.4 (released 2007) introduces input/output
event handling based on high-performance primitives: BSD kqueue (also
present in MacOS X), Linux epoll, and Solaris /dev/poll.  These implement
more scalable event handling than the older select() and poll() primitives.

With 2.6 Linux kernels, Postfix 2.4 and later has an epoll file descriptor
leak when it executes non-Postfix commands in, for example, a user's
$HOME/.forward file.  A local user can access a leaked epoll file descriptor
to implement a denial of service attack on Postfix. The attack may result in
reduced Postfix performance, or in automatic Postfix shutdown when an
internal safety mechanism triggers.  Some possible attacks are discussed in
the last paragraph of this section.

Not affected is Postfix input/output event handling based on BSD kqueue and
Solaris /dev/poll.  There, the kernel effectively revokes access to the
underlying kernel object when it creates a child process with fork(),
keeping the kernel object normally accessible only by the process that
creates it.

The above approaches could help to improve the consistency of Linux
input/output event notification.  Currently, 1) different Linux processes
may make conflicting updates to a shared epoll instance; 2) therefore, the
Linux kernel may report input/output events to processes that didn't ask for
those events; and 3) those events may involve activity on pipes, sockets,
etc.  that aren't open in those processes.  Such inconsistency could be
avoided when an epoll instance were normally accessible only by the process
that creates it.

Workaround:
===========
Allow only trusted users to control delivery to non-Postfix commands. In the
following example, the directory /var/forward is not writable by users, and
Postfix is configured to search for /var/forward/username (plus optional
address extension) instead of the default location
~username/.forward (plus optional address extension).

/etc/postfix/main.cf:
     forward_path = /var/forward/${user}${recipient_delimiter}${extension},
 	/var/forward/${user}

Other workarounds would be required for other mail filtering software that
executes commands in user-controlled configuration files.

Solution:
=========
Apply the source code patch below, or install an updated Postfix version. 
Postfix versions 2.4.9, 2.5.5, and 2.6-20080902 are made available via
http://www.postfix.org/. Vendors will make updated versions available
according to their own support policies.

Patch:
======

Begin of patch
*** src/util/events.c.orig	Mon Mar 24 13:19:23 2008
--- src/util/events.c	Tue Aug 26 17:43:41 2008
***************
*** 426,431 ****
--- 427,433 ----

   #define EVENT_REG_INIT_HANDLE(er, n) do { \
   	er = event_epollfd = epoll_create(n); \
+ 	if (event_epollfd >= 0) close_on_exec(event_epollfd, CLOSE_ON_EXEC); \
       } while (0)
   #define EVENT_REG_INIT_TEXT	"epoll_create"

End of patch

came across this in the README for SASL:

"IMPORTANT: pwcheck establishes a UNIX domain socket in /var/pwcheck and

read+execute permission to this directory or authentication attempts 
will fail."

From: http://www.postfix.org/SASL_README.html

From teknotus at gmail.com  Wed Sep  3 10:55:38 2008
From: teknotus at gmail.com (Daniel Johnson)
Date: Wed, 3 Sep 2008 10:55:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] video TO and keyboard/mouse FROM a laptop
In-Reply-To: <48AC865A.1000400@acm.org>
References: <48AC865A.1000400@acm.org>
Message-ID: <ec0b5e080809031055i359e4c0cg30cd9b6c0b727146@mail.gmail.com>

> Is there any kind of an adapter to let me use a laptop as the video,
> keyboard and mouse for another computer.  I've seen mention of network
> based KVM switches but would only need/want to connect to one system at
> a time.

I want to second the person who mentioned IPMI.  I can't recommend
this strongly enough.

Most recent servers have at least IPMI 1.5, and almost any new one
will have the 2.0 spec.

At my last job we setup IPMI to give us serial over Lan and got it
working with the bios screen.  So it was like being able to ssh to the
console.  IPMI also lets you do things like turn the power off, and
on.  We setup a PXE server in the colo box.  With the combination of
IPMI, serial over lan, and PXE boot images we could fix almost
anything that wasn't a hardware issue without traveling to the colo.
It was a bit of a learning curve, but well worth it.

From saharabeara at gmail.com  Wed Sep  3 11:20:54 2008
From: saharabeara at gmail.com (Sarah A Sharp)
Date: Wed, 3 Sep 2008 11:20:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Looking for volunteers for LPC Student Day
Message-ID: <4c8f91e80809031120x69629d9cxd11cd0dd949d5146@mail.gmail.com>

Greetings,

I'm looking for volunteers to be on an open source career panel, and
a volunteer to speak about debugging at the Linux Plumbers Conference
Student Day.

The debugging talk would be about 45 minutes, and cover the basics of
debugging the Linux kernel and open source applications.  Some of the
content could be taken from the debugging chapter in Linux Device
Drivers.  The talk could cover printk, dmesg and other interesting logs
in /var/log, common oops messages, debugfs, sysfs, debugging over serial
ports, etc.  Let me know if you're willing to volunteer to give this
talk, or if you have past slides that someone else could use.

We're also looking for representatives from companies who contribute to
open source and open source consulants to be a part of the career panel
at the Linux Plumbers Conference Student Day.  We will have about 20
students in the audience who will ask questions, and we will have some
prepared questions in case students are shy.

Student Day takes place on September 16th.  For more information, see:
	http://linuxplumbersconf.org/program/students/

Sarah Sharp

Great!  You're based in Boston, right?  This would mean that you would
have to be in Portland a day earlier (Tuesday) than the main LPC
conference (which starts on Wednesday).  I wasn't sure whether you would
be in town for Kernel Summit already, and I want to make sure there's no
misunderstandings.

> Debugging the kernel and debugging applications are quite different. The
> kernel is a bit more complex, whereas it is easy to use gdb for user space
> applications. There is kgdb, but I seldom use it. I would focus on how to
> read an oops, using printk, serial ports (minicom and ttywatch), kallsyms,
> SystemMap (for those that forgot to compile with kallsyms), ftrace, logdev
> (my open source internal reader).  I wouldn't go too much over sysfs since
> that isn't too helpful for debugging. Debugfs is usually for those that are
> developing something in the kernel and want to interact with the user space
> (this is what ftrace uses).

Honestly, the list I put together was off the top of my head, and I'll
defer to your experience in debugging.

From wevl at pacifier.com  Fri Sep  5 10:08:09 2008
From: wevl at pacifier.com (Wayne E. Van Loon Sr.)

Subject: [PLUG] Can anyone here repair a motherboard trace?

References: <48C0C6E1.9040200@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <48C16779.5070303@pacifier.com>

m0gely:
At several stints as repair technician / field engineer, I have repaired 
many circuit boards, usually required after removing an IC. I repair 
them with a short run of wire-wrap wire soldered at each side of the 
break. You have to be very careful doing this as too much heat will 
cause very fine traces will lift off the board. I would recommend using 
as low a wattage soldering iron as possible. Any chance you could take a 
photograph of the trace that needs repair and attach it to an email?

If you don't get a better offer, you can bring it by my house and I 
would be glad to do it for you as a favor. I work at home (Beaverton OR) 
much of the time and am today.

Wayne
( Industrial Automation keywords at PLUG last night)

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Fri Sep  5 19:17:05 2008
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Fri, 5 Sep 2008 20:17:05 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Perl question
In-Reply-To: <91692AD9790B4A85948969CD7CFBF83A@RAMA>
References: <91692AD9790B4A85948969CD7CFBF83A@RAMA>
Message-ID: <18625.59425.354877.788232@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>

William A Morita writes:
 > I was translating a bash script to perl and encountered this dilemma:
 > 
 > In bash I can do
 > ScriptDir=$(cd $(dirname $0) ; pwd)
 > 
 > I tried doing the equivalent in perl, but the sticking point seems to be the
 > ";" in the middle of the compound statement.
 > 
 > Maybe I'm overlooking something obvious here.
 > 
 > Some suggestions guys?
 > 
 > - Bill 

Not a perl hacker, so I may be way off base here, especially where
idiomatic style is concerned.

chdir() returns true or false.  It would be handier if it returned the
new directory's pathname.  Then it could be used functionally:

$scriptDir = chdir($0);

But maybe this would work:

$scriptDir = (chdir($0) && `pwd`);

Then $scriptDir is null if the operation failed, which gives you some
useful feedback.

Is chdir() sticky like in PHP, where even if you call it in a
function, you stay in the new directory even after the function exits,
as if it's changing the current directory for the entire script
process?
-- 
Carlos


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri Sep  5 21:33:57 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 5 Sep 2008 21:33:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Perl question
In-Reply-To: <91692AD9790B4A85948969CD7CFBF83A@RAMA>
References: <91692AD9790B4A85948969CD7CFBF83A@RAMA>
Message-ID: <200809052133.58130.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi Bill,

# from William A Morita
# on Friday 05 September 2008 18:56:

>ScriptDir=$(cd $(dirname $0) ; pwd)
>
>I tried doing the equivalent in perl, but the sticking point seems to
> be the ";" in the middle of the compound statement.

What equivalent has ';' in the middle of it?  Running shell commands for 
that sort of thing is usually not desirable.

  use File::Fu;
  my $script_dir = File::Fu->file($0)->dirname->absolute;

Would be equivalent to:

  use File::Basename qw(dirname);
  use File::Spec;
  my $script_dir = File::Spec->rel2abs(dirname($0));

Or, if you want to eliminate all of the possible ../ parts:

  use File::Basename qw(dirname)
  use Cwd qw(abs_path);
  my $script_dir = abs_path(dirname($0));

But perl won't stop you from doing shell expansion expansion within your 
shell expansions as long as you get your sigils properly escaped:

  my $script_dir = qx(cd \$(dirname $0) ; pwd)

Though you should remember to chomp() the result and check $?;

  chomp(my $script_dir = qx(cd \$(dirname $0) ; pwd)); die $! if($?);

And you might just get the current directory back if the cd fails, so:

  chomp(my $script_dir = qx(cd \$(dirname $0) && pwd)); die $! if($?);

But you could get your home directory back if dirname fails, so, uh...

  chomp(my $script_dir =
    qx(cd \$(dirname $0 || echo /dev/null) && pwd)); die $! if($?);

But I recommend File::Fu.  If you want the cwd-style result with no 
dots, I could add that feature.

Finally, don't use FindBin.

--Eric

From barryb at proaxis.com  Sat Sep  6 19:01:46 2008
From: barryb at proaxis.com (Bill Barry)
Date: Sat, 6 Sep 2008 19:01:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Bigmem not recognizing memory
Message-ID: <af070b010809061901r5d88911dqe9e1f1ae95ab7be5@mail.gmail.com>

On Tue, Jul 22, 2008 at 1:53 PM, Bill Barry <barryb at proaxis.com> wrote:
> I recently added 4GB of ram to a machine that already had 2GB for a total of
> 6GB of memory, but I only see 4GB in  /proc/meminfo.  The bios shows that
> the memory is recognized and is functioning, but something is still wrong.
> Here is some relavant information.
>
> :~# cat /proc/meminfo
> MemTotal:      4117296 kB
> MemFree:        405400 kB
>
> I am using a stock Debian bigmem kernel:
>  2.6.25-2-686-bigmem.
>
> The processor is:
> CPU1: AMD Athlon(tm) 64 X2 Dual Core Processor 4000+ stepping 01
>
> The motherboard is:
> ASUS M2NPV-VM GF6150 AM2
>
> Thanks for any help,
> Bill Barry
>
>

Well, I finally figured out what why I could only access 4GB of my 6GB
of memory.  It turned out that I had installed the memory wrong. This
motherboard has 4 memory slots in 2 banks, A and B. I had installed 2
1GB DIMMs in bank A and 2 2GB DIMMs in bank B, but as a last resort I
read the manual  which says that A1+B1 must equal A2+B2, i.e. I had to
install 3 GB in each bank to make them symmetric.   So the bigmem
kernel does work, I can access 6GB with a 32 bit kernel, with each
process  limited to less than 4GB. As part of  trying to find out
where the problem was I also installed a partition with a 64 bit
kernel and I am slowly migrating everything over to that.

Thanks Alan, Jason, Drew and Derek for your suggestions.

Bill

Message-ID: <200809062354.49638.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Larry Brigman
# on Saturday 06 September 2008 22:10:

>> Finally, don't use FindBin.
>
>Why not? ?If you have module files in the same directory as the perl
>script then it is useful.

You have module files in /usr/local/bin?

You can use it if you want, but make sure all of your users read 
the 'BUGS' section in the documentation and/or be very careful what you 
name your script.

By the way, are you using the symlink resolution feature?

The next release of File::Fu will have File::Fu->program_name 
and ->program_dir (in trunk now), and I suppose ->resolve should just 
be a method on the basic object (so you only pay for that when you use 
it.)

I'm not sure where FindBin acquired this misfeature of searching $PATH 
when the script is obviously *right in front of you*, but I suspect it 
either had something to do with a dead OS or some other bit of 
interesting/useless historical trivia.  Onions in the varnish, cutting 
the ends off of the ham, standing the game console on end, etc.

--Eric

After a lengthy battle, I got DKIM working on my mail server.  I'm
going to have to document my efforts to save others from the pain.
Lots of little gotchas and what-have-yous.  But that is not what this
email is about.

a test mail to a reflector (check-auth at verifier dot port25 dot
com).  But yahoo doesn't recognize my DKIM signature at all.  The
thing yahoo calls DKIM must still be the antiquated DomainKeys.  What
a bummer, since the entire reason I went through the hassle of setting
up DKIM was to enable my users to send email to yahoo.

My questions:

- Is yahoo going to bring their DKIM implementation up to speed any
  time soon, or will they languish in yesteryear for as long as
  possible, like they did with BIND8?

- Can dkim-milter-2.7.0 be made to work with yahoo's implementation?

- I just now got DKIM working properly.  Do I need to wait a day
  before writing off yahoo?  Might they be caching my previous failed
  attempts?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Sep  8 09:39:38 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 8 Sep 2008 09:39:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Log file translation to English
Message-ID: <20080908093938.e96c8ae5.johnxj@comcast.net>

First, the long story:

I have periodic problems with my Hardy x86_64 locking up on my T61 Thinkpad. That is, the lockups are sometimes a bit different, but in all cases the only solution is to reboot. For example, this morning moving the mouse (bluetooth) failed to restore the screen from the screensaver. Now, the bluetooth mouse has always suffered from random disconnects. Usually I get several days before it suddenly quits working. I turned off the touchpad in the BIOS a long time ago but left the trackball working so I have a "backup mouse" when that happens. I just open a terminal and do "sudo hidd --connect <address> after holding the mouse "look for me" button down for 12 seconds. It's annoying, but I suffer it because I love the mouse.

This morning when the mouse failed to restore the screen I tried Ctrl-Alt-F1, which restored the screen, although it did not go to a command line. I tried the mouse again and discovered that it had died again. That would explain why moving it did not restore the screen. Intending to reconnect it I used the trackball to go to Applications > Accessories > Terminal, but I was unable to open a terminal with the trackball. The panel said "opening terminal" for a bit, then nothing. I tried Ctrl-Alt-F1 to get to a command line, but discovered that the keyboard had died. The trackball would select menu items, but I could not restore any programs that had been minimized to the panel. Eventually the trackball wouldn't even select menu items. At that point I had to reboot.

This morning's episode is a bit different because usually the lockups are complete - no keyboard, no mouse, no trackball - nada. I should also mention that I have never ever had the computer lock up while using it. It is always when I return to the computer after leaving it for several hours, only to discover that it has locked up. 

I can also add that it sits on a docking station at home, which is plugged into the wall. However, I have a desktop that is also plugged into the wall (no UPS because it is used only for backups and for watching movies). In the past when I discovered the T61 had locked up I found that the desktop was completely shut down. In other words, it was PGE's fault. A momentary power outage had occurred in my absence, although it must have been very brief because the stereo was still playing. I asked on the Thinkpad e-list about this because it didn't make sense to me for such a brief power outage to affect the Thinkpad. If the mains go down it should automatically switch to the battery without incident. The response on the e-list was that it was probably a power surge. I got no "me too" responses from other T61 users, so evidently my problem is unique.

Now the question:

In my several years of using Ubuntu I have never paid much attention to log files. This morning I decided it was time to rectify that failure in my Linux education, in the hope that I could figure out what is causing the lockups. After a bit of googling I figured out that /var/log/messages (and its .0, .1 ... prior copies) would be the best place to find answers. I opened both /messages and /messages.0 in gedit, but found that I was unable to scrute the messages. For example, I found this in /messages at the time I gave up and hit the power button:

<pages of stuff deleted>
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438891]  [system_call+0x7e/0x83] system_call+0x7e/0x83
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438923] 
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438926] 
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438927] Code: 48 8b 73 38 48 8b 3d 09 8c 39 00 48 89 d9 48 c7 c2 10 38 30 
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438956]  RSP <ffff810069df5a58>
Sep  8 08:20:29 Devil7 kernel: [160176.438967] ---[ end trace 47b9677cdef171ba ]---
Sep  8 08:25:46 Devil7 syslogd 1.5.0#1ubuntu1: restart.
<more pages showing evidently the boot process>

Looking at the pages of stuff before the "restart" line I find nothing that I can understand. I would assume that the poking around that I did with the trackball left messages, but I am unable to find them.

I also find this at intervals of exactly 20 minutes, apparently while the computer is idle:

Sep  7 12:10:08 Devil7 -- MARK --
Sep  7 12:30:08 Devil7 -- MARK --

What is that all about?

Is there any way to figure out what the pages of inscrutable messages mean?

Are there any other log files that I should be looking at to troubleshoot this issue?

On Wednesday 27 August 2008 07:49:03 pm Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> On Wed, Aug 27, 2008 at 05:17:32PM -0700, Aaron Ten Clay wrote:
> > If you know anyone else getting FiOS, you might want to recommend they
> > have ethernet run into their home instead of coax. That's what I did.
> > They won't charge extra at install time and it's much easier to remove
> > the actiontec router - just unplug the cat5e and connect to a new linux
> > PC for routing :)
>
> I had the cat5 there, waiting to plug into the ONT, but the installer
> insisted on running coax to an Actiontec.  Friendly enough fellow,
> but it had to be done his way.  The coax is needed to feed video,
> and MoCA is needed for video-on-demand, and the installer couldn't
> imagine anyone surviving without a boob tube.

Running a 30/15mbit FiOS line here with unlimited phone + high def TV. Had to 
call tech support to get the Ethernet port on the ONT enabled, wired up my 
own Slackware-based router, and plugged the WAN port on the MoCA router into 
the switch connected to the LAN side of the Slackware router. Basically: ONT 
Eth -> Slackware Router -> Switch -> MoCA router -> FiOS boxes running over 
coax. So far everything seems to be working fine, Video on Demand, Guides, 
etc.. with the added bit of fun of being able to use tcpdump to capture the 
MPEG-TS stream used for the Video on Demand content along with Guide 
information.

Thanks,
	- Devin

/me makes choking noises

If the card doesn't work for you with native drivers, you might want to
consider just replacing the internal card with one that has a decent
chipset.  If it's a mini-PCI card, this is typically very easy in
laptops and there's tons of cheap cards on eBay.  You could also use a
PCMCIA or ExpressCard adapter until Linux gets stable drivers for the
built-in.

good luck,
tim

I agree with Tim: use something that's linux compatible, ideally
replacing the existing card.

Another option in that direction is USB wireless.  I volunteer for a
group the refurbished machines, something similar to FreeGeek.  We go
some machines that didn't have built-in wifi. So I tried out some USB
wifi sticks.  Below is my post to the groups mailing list.  One
caveat: although both worked on the machines we had in the shop, one
stick didn't work in my Lenovo machine which had a built-in wifi card.

Regards,
- Robert

---------- Forwarded message ----------
From: Robert Citek <robert.citek at gmail.com>
Date: Sat, Sep 6, 2008 at 9:57 PM
Subject: Re: PIII machines from WITS
To: Bworks_shop <bworks_shop at yahoogroups.com>


These machines do not have built-in wifi nor room enough to put in a
full-height PCI card.  So I picked up two different USB wifi sticks at
BestBuy to try out.

Belkin - F5D7050 v4001 [1]
LinkSys - WUSB54GC [2]

Both worked flawlessly on an open wifi.  Plug in.  Wait.  Click on the
NetworkManager applet to choose wifi network.  While both cards worked
on the machines from WITS, the Linksys did not work on my Lenovo
laptop, which has built-in wifi.  Even when turned off, the Linksys
USB wifi did not work on my laptop.

Both cards cost about $40 at BestBuy.  Might be able to get them for
less elsewhere.

More details on supported USB wifi cards at Ubuntu's website [3].

[1] https://help.ubuntu.com/community/HardwareSupportComponentsWirelessNetworkCardsBelkin#USB

[2] https://help.ubuntu.com/community/HardwareSupportComponentsWirelessNetworkCardsLinksys#USB

[3] https://help.ubuntu.com/community/WifiDocs/WirelessCardsSupported

Regards,
- Robert


From plug at merlot.com  Wed Sep 10 15:47:45 2008
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)

Subject: [PLUG] interesting data transfer bug
Message-ID: <20080910224745.GA16040@merlot.com>

I have a few servers behind a small NetScreen firewall in a colo. They
are running Debian, with one runnign Ubuntu Hardy Server. All of them
have trouble transferring large files on their public interfaces, but no
problems transferring files on the back net. 

I see this problem when apt-getting a new kernel (or other file larger
than about 2M. though sometimes smaller files won't go either), rsyncing
a directory full of mail archives, and using wget to download tarballs.
I have had a little success with aria2c, which seems to restart the
download a little while after it stalls.

I suspect the firewall, but I would like to find the problem before
buying a new firewall.

Where should I begin? I can use the text wireshark, tcpdump, or similar,
but then I'll end up with a large file that needs to be grepped (for
what?) because I won't be able to get it off the system without lots of
restarts.

Thanks!

> but then I'll end up with a large file that needs to be grepped (for
> what?) because I won't be able to get it off the system without  
> lots of
> restarts.

So, only keep the last chunk of data? ;)

Wireshark:
-----
-b <capture ring buffer option>
     Cause Wireshark to run in ``multiple files'' mode. In ``multiple  
files'' mode, Wireshark will write to several capture files. When the  
first capture file fills up, Wireshark will switch writing to the  
next file and so on.

     The created filenames are based on the filename given with the - 
w flag, the number of the file and on the creation date and time,  
e.g. outfile_00001_20050604120117.pcap,  
outfile_00001_20050604120523.pcap, ...

     With the files option it's also possible to form a ``ring  
buffer''. This will fill up new files until the number of files  
specified, at which point Wireshark will discard the data in the  
first file and start writing to that file and so on. If the files  
option is not set, new files filled up until one of the capture stop  
conditions match (or until the disk if full).

     The criterion is of the form key:value, where key is one of:

     duration:value switch to the next file after value seconds have  
elapsed, even if the current file is not completely filled up.

     filesize:value switch to the next file after it reaches a size  
of value kilobytes (where a kilobyte is 1024 bytes).

     files:value begin again with the first file after value number  
of files were written (form a ring buffer).
--------
(from http://www.wireshark.org/docs/man-pages/wireshark.html)

tcpdump:
----
-s
     Snarf snaplen bytes of data from each packet rather than the  
default of 68 (with SunOS's NIT, the minimum is actually 96). 68  
bytes is adequate for IP, ICMP, TCP and UDP but may truncate protocol  
information from name server and NFS packets (see below). Packets  
truncated because of a limited snapshot are indicated in the output  
with ``[|proto]'', where proto is the name of the protocol level at  
which the truncation has occurred. Note that taking larger snapshots  
both increases the amount of time it takes to process packets and,  
effectively, decreases the amount of packet buffering. This may cause  
packets to be lost. You should limit snaplen to the smallest number  
that will capture the protocol information you're interested in.  
Setting snaplen to 0 means use the required length to catch whole  
packets.

-C
     Before writing a raw packet to a savefile, check whether the  
file is currently larger than file_size and, if so, close the current  
savefile and open a new one. Savefiles after the first savefile will  
have the name specified with the -w flag, with a number after it,  
starting at 1 and continuing upward. The units of file_size are  
millions of bytes (1,000,000 bytes, not 1,048,576 bytes).

-w
     Write the raw packets to file rather than parsing and printing  
them out. They can later be printed with the -r option. Standard  
output is used if file is ``-''.
----
(from http://www.tcpdump.org/tcpdump_man.html )

....Then you only have to move/look at the last file or so. :)

Good luck!

-Bop

Thanks. That's useful. So just for fun, I ran the Wireshark GUI on my
laptop, and discovered something interesting: it appears to be a problem
with the laptop, probably a bad NIC. 

I see a ton of bad checksums (the incorrect checksum is mostly the same:
0xaeaf) and some chaos that seems to be a result of those (duplicate
ACKs, mostly).

Since I have a pile of images that need to be uploaded, I tried from my
laptop, captured a trace, then burned a CD and sent the images
(successfully) from another PC in my office.

Fortunately my laptop is still under warrantee, but unfortunately I
don't know how to prove it's the hardware other than to describe how
different version of software all fail. It failed when plugged into the
laptop's RJ port, and it fails when the laptop is docked and the cable
is attached to the dock. There's only one ethernet chip, so this is not
a surprise.

I was running the latest Hardy kernel when I wrote the original email to
the list, and I am running the Intrepid 64-bit kernel as I type this. I
still have the same problem. So it's not likely to be a kernel or driver
issue; it's an Intel gigabit NIC, and I can't believe that checksum bugs
would persist from 2.6.24 to 2.6.27. 

Anyone got the T61 diagnostic CD?

Thanks.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Wed Sep 10 21:50:03 2008
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Wed, 10 Sep 2008 21:50:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] interesting data transfer bug
In-Reply-To: <20080910224745.GA16040@merlot.com>
References: <20080910224745.GA16040@merlot.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0809102150m1ad11793sf37ef365822e5c49@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, Sep 10, 2008 at 3:47 PM, Kurt Sussman <plug at merlot.com> wrote:
> I have a few servers behind a small NetScreen firewall in a colo. They
> are running Debian, with one runnign Ubuntu Hardy Server. All of them
> have trouble transferring large files on their public interfaces, but no
> problems transferring files on the back net.
>

One possibility is TCP window scaling.  The TCP option improves
performance on high-bandwidth high-latency links.  It was enabled by
default in recent versions of the Linux kernel.  Some older firewalls
get really confused by the option and drop connections.

You can try disabling through the /proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_window_scaling file.
You might try doing "echo 0 > /proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_window_scaling"
and see if that helps.  You can disable it permanently in the
/etc/sysctl.conf file.

 - Ian

I'm looking at the necessity of rewiring all of my interior phone
lines at home, as the ugly mess of 1950s, 60s, and 70s-era wiring will
no longer support a reasonably high-speed DSL line.

I want to install a DSL/POTS splitter at our NID or just inside the
house from it, rather than relying on DSL filters at each phone set.
I'm going to replace all of the mix of plastic insulated, cloth
insulated, and bare wiring with CAT5.

I'm looking for suggestions for a local, reasonably-priced supplier
here in town for a big spool of CAT-5 wire, crimp-on RJ-11 and RJ-45
ends, the DSL splitter, and miscellaneous phone junction boxes, wall
jacks, etc.

I may end up needing to go to multiple places to get everything I
need. I would guess Frys would be cheapest for the CAT5 and the
crimp-on ends, but I'm not sure they'd have DSL/POTS splitters. I also
might find the phone junction boxes and wall jacks cheaper at a
hardware store.

I'll pretty well need to do most all of this tonight, or else I'll be
in big trouble with my Internet-deprived wife and daughter.

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Sat Sep 13 20:41:28 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sat, 13 Sep 2008 20:41:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Deciphering e-mail headers
In-Reply-To: <20080913190159.3a23536d.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080913190159.3a23536d.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20080914034128.GA2472@sentinelchicken.org>

Hi,



> I have several e-mail accounts, all of which go to the same Inbox in
> Sylpheed, my mail client. (No, I don't want them to go to different
> Inboxes.) Today I received a spam e-mail. The complete headers are
> below.



First off, the destination email address specified by an SMTP client is
not given in the email headers (which appear in the SMTP DATA section),
but rather in the RCPT command(s) which occur earlier in the SMTP
conversation.  This and other information is sometimes referred to the
envelope.  Mail servers just deliver what's in the DATA section (or what
could be thought of as what's inside the envelope) and not the envelope
itself.  Some mail servers are nice enough to add a "Delivered-to"
header which contains what it saw in the envelope as the delivery
address. In your case I don't see one, which means you have to guess.

To help answer your question, mail servers should stamp messages with at
least one Received header as they forward the message to the next
destination.  These are stamped in reverse chronological order, so the
newest Received lines are at the top.

Given the last Received header:

> Received: from imta27.emeryville.ca.mail.comcast.net ([76.96.30.95])
>           by alnrmxc21.comcast.net (alnrmxc21) with ESMTP
>           id <20080913234021a2100jfmnqe>; Sat, 13 Sep 2008 23:40:21 +0000

I'm guessing that one of your email accounts is a comcast account?  If
so, then it would be a safe bet that this is where it came in to.  If
you have multiple Comcast accounts delivering to the same inbox, then
you're probably SoL when it comes to determining which.

HTH,
tim

Subject: [PLUG] How to Block WEB Access for Certain IPs?
Message-ID: <48CE63DC.1060807@gisnet.com>

I'm trying to block a couple of overly busy Chinese web spiders from 
sniffing my site every hour, but not having much luck. I first tried 
putting this in /etc/hosts.deny:

ALL: 61.135.168.39,  220.181.32.22

but that didn't stop them. Then I tried putting this in 
/etc/http/conf/httpd.conf in the <Directory> tag:
    Order allow,deny
    Allow from all
    Deny from 61.135.168.39  220.181.32.22

and that didn't work. I restarted httpd each time I made a change. I'm 
using Fedora Core 9.

So how do you block a particular IP from accessing your web site? 
(without disturbing the firewall if possible.)

- Bill Thoen

> but that didn't stop them. Then I tried putting this in 
> /etc/http/conf/httpd.conf in the <Directory> tag:
>     Order allow,deny
>     Allow from all
>     Deny from 61.135.168.39  220.181.32.22

Apache's order/allow/deny directives can be incredibly confusing (at
least they are for me).  Every time I mess with them I have to guess and
check my ordering just to make sure it's right.  Have you tried:

     Order deny,allow
     Deny from 61.135.168.39  220.181.32.22
     Allow from all

??

What does the rest of your Directory block look like?  Is that tag for
the document root?  Do you have any other Directory blocks that might
override that behavior for specific areas?

HTH,
tim

I use this scheme to log then drop the bad guys:

iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -j LOG --log-prefix "IPTABLES DROPPED"
iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -j DROP

frank hunt
befuddled linux admin
erstwhile photographer
hillsboro oregon

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Mon Sep 15 09:24:56 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Mon, 15 Sep 2008 10:24:56 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] How to Block WEB Access for Certain IPs?
In-Reply-To: <48CE84B3.3040107@frankhunt.com>
References: <48CE63DC.1060807@gisnet.com>  <48CE84B3.3040107@frankhunt.com>
Message-ID: <1221495896.9053.138.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Mon, 2008-09-15 at 08:52 -0700, frank hunt wrote:
> iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -j LOG --log-prefix "IPTABLES DROPPED"
> iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -j DROP

Netfilter logging can be dangerous. If you're going to do it, I
recommend adding rate limiting to protect against accidental or
intentional DoS. For example:

iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -m limit --limit 1/minute -j LOG
iptables -A INPUT -s $IP -j DROP

man iptables(8) for details

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Sep 15 11:51:57 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Mon, 15 Sep 2008 11:51:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting Wed Sept 17th 2008
Message-ID: <1221504717.5827.16.camel@rotwang>

PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting

Date:	Wednesday September 17th, 2008
Time:   7:00pm - ~9:00pm 

Location:  Jax Bar
           826 SW 2nd Ave 
           Portland, OR 97204-3089
           http://www.jaxbar.com/

Speaker:   brian d foy <brian at stonehenge.com>

Topic:     Indexing CPAN

BackPAN is the historical archive of the Comprehensive Perl Archive
Network, where most Perl modules live. Going back to 1993, BackPAN has
about 100,000 distributions, taking up 12 GB of space. So far, there
has not been a comprehensive index of all of these distributions. I'm
working on a project to go through each distribution, record as much
information as I can, and store that in a way that other people can
ask questions. The big goal now is to take any Perl module file you
have installed locally and query my index to determine exactly which
distribution it came from, when it was released, what other files came
with it, and at the time of it's release what its dependency list was.

My talk is a demonstration of what I have so far, which is still in
the data collection phase. I have an indexer that knows how to go
through distributions and record information, and can do some with
pluggable components for each portion of the work. In my talk, I'll
demonstrate the current indexer and talk a little bit about the tools
and techniques I use. There's plenty of cool Perl things going on, and
I'm sure some of the Linux people will have valuable comments about
software indexing.

On Mon, 2008-09-15 at 10:38 -0700, wes wrote:
> I have this same problem periodically. SuSe appears to subscribe to the
> philosophy that "the latest is the greatest" - upgrade or get left behind.

SUSE 10.1 is no longer supported. No bug fixes. No security updates.
Zero. Zilch. Nada.

You should not be using SUSE 10.1 on a system connected to the Internet.
If you choose to use a free distro, you choose to be forced to upgrade
regularly.

If you want to be able to use an ancient SUSE release, pay for a SLES
support contract. Or pay for RHEL. Or switch to CentOS. Ubuntu LTS
doesn't have as long a support period, but it's still longer than
openSUSE, Fedora or normal Ubuntu.

(Once upon a time Debian was a good distro for people that refused to
upgrade, but now that Debian has gotten better about releasing every
couple years it also has a more limited support period.)

From sjansen at buscaluz.org  Tue Sep 16 08:11:29 2008
From: sjansen at buscaluz.org (Stuart Jansen)
Date: Tue, 16 Sep 2008 09:11:29 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] SUSE 10.1 and YaST
In-Reply-To: <b399d1c10809160735o7648b401gdb166b404e49cfc0@mail.gmail.com>
References: <48CE9565.6070502@comcast.net>
	<b399d1c10809151038o205ac594ye8ac4b8473d8903d@mail.gmail.com>
	<1221529216.7412.6.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
	<b399d1c10809152125j33e626el9d034da0041bc3c@mail.gmail.com>
	<1221541541.7412.13.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>
	<b399d1c10809160735o7648b401gdb166b404e49cfc0@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1221577889.7412.36.camel@simplicity.buscaluz.org>

On Tue, 2008-09-16 at 07:35 -0700, wes wrote:
> My beef is more that the packages are so version-dependent. This is nothing
> short of built-in obsolescence.

This is nothing short of a deliberate technical decision. Most of the
Linux community values access to source code over backwards
compatibility. Package version requirements are merely a reflection of
the reality that compatibility can not be assumed, it must be tested.
Doing so is expensive. Commercial options like RHEL and SLES provide
that service by backporting bug fixes, security updates, and even
certain features. Free distros simply don't have the resources.

Good or bad, the Linux community does things differently than the
proprietary world or even the BSDs. The Linux method is not designed to
make life hard for you, it is a collection of deliberate technical and
cultural responses to the complexity of software development.

> > I repeat, if you wanted long term support you should have payed for it.
> > Either with a support subscription, or else with your own hardware and
> > bandwidth.
> 
> Don't suppose you happen to have a DIY package repository howto handy...

Sixty seconds of quality time with Google turned up the following:
http://en.opensuse.org/Mirror_Setup_Howto
http://en.opensuse.org/Mirror_Infrastructure

Of course that's for a full fledged mirror. If all you want is personal
use, gather all the RPMs into a directory. Use createrepo to turn it
into a YUM repository. Register the repository with rug (rug sa).
Subscribe to the repository (rug subscribe).

I'm not interested in spending any more of my time writing a more
detailed set of instructions or searching for a better set of
instructions. It isn't hard, but I don't want to do enough work that you
start feeling I also owe you my free services.



From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Sep 16 08:26:28 2008

On Tue, 16 Sep 2008, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> ... things will happen to my home directory.

Dick,

   They shouldn't if /home is on a separate partition. Of course, you should
always back up those file systems you want to restore as they were.

> How do I know which things I've added, like VMware Server, will survive
> the upgrade, and which ones won't? This time around I have a mostly empty
> large hard drive added to the machine where I can put a complete copy of
> /home/rsteff -- I could even put a complete copy of the drive with SuSE
> 10.1 on it. If I do that, will it help recover from changes I don't know
> will happen?

   That will help. As a practice, whatever you install yourself that's not
part of the distribution should go in either /usr/local or /opt. Those are
not overwritten by the installation of a different distribution. At least,
not the most famous ones.

From timb at bruce4.com  Tue Sep 16 09:59:56 2008
From: timb at bruce4.com (Tim Bruce)
Date: Tue, 16 Sep 2008 09:59:56 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Preparing for a distro change
In-Reply-To: <48CFBE49.2010108@comcast.net>
References: <48CFBE49.2010108@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <55265.192.136.50.130.1221584396.squirrel@sm.tbruce.com>

Dick,

Using the command
    rpm -qa

under SuSE Linux should list all your INSTALLED packages.  That will give
you an idea of what software you need to reinstall (other than new
dependencies).

If /home is installed on another partition, you don't have to worry about
that being impacted by the new install.  However, you should back it up. 
A new Linux version max expect certain config files (or new parameters) in
your personal directory and may not work correctly.  If you're staying
with SuSE, there's been some discussion about that with the new version of
KDE, but I haven't followed all the conversations.

I'd also backup /etc and tuck that away into a new location to ensure you
don't overwrite anything important there (after tweaking your system).

Best of luck.

Tim
-- 
Timothy J. Bruce

visit my Website at: http://www.tbruce.com
Registered Linux User #325725

From dpape at dpape.com  Tue Sep 16 16:49:27 2008
From: dpape at dpape.com (Daniel Pape)
Date: Tue, 16 Sep 2008 16:49:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Preparing for a distro change
In-Reply-To: <48D03071.6020502@comcast.net>
References: <48CFBE49.2010108@comcast.net>	<55265.192.136.50.130.1221584396.squirrel@sm.tbruce.com>
	<48D03071.6020502@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <48D04607.3050700@dpape.com>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Tim Bruce wrote:
>> Using the command
>>     rpm -qa
>>
>> under SuSE Linux should list all your INSTALLED packages.  That will give
>> you an idea of what software you need to reinstall (other than new
>> dependencies).
>>   
> Yes, it gave me quite a list. I pointed the output of the command to a
> text file. There are 910 lines in that file. While I haven't gone over
> every line, I do recognize some of the file names. I suspect that's not
> the productive way to approach the problem, but I'm glad to know it exists.

You can also try

  rpm -qa --last

which will give you installation dates and order the packages from most
to least recently installed. It might help you narrow down the "special"
ones which you have installed since the original system creation.

Slightly OT: rpm also supports a bunch of other reporting options... for
instance, this command will tell you how much disk space each
installation is taking up, and you can sort it from biggest to smallest:

  rpm -qa --queryformat "%-15{SIZE} %{NAME}\n"  | sort -nr



dan





From david.fleck at mchsi.com  Wed Sep 17 06:27:23 2008
From: david.fleck at mchsi.com (David Fleck)
Date: Wed, 17 Sep 2008 08:27:23 -0500 (CDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Hardware puzzle of the day - desktop won't boot
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0809170807090.29169@grond.sourballs.org>

Seeking advice/opinions on the following situation:

6-year old homebuilt PC, ASUS motherboard, Athlon CPU, OpenSuSE 10.1 (not 
that I think that part is relevant.)

About a week ago, I came downstairs and discovered the machine was off.  
It was on when I left it the previous evening.

I pushded the button, and the fans whirred up. No other activity - no 
POST, no HDD noise or lights.

I thought that maybe the power supply was bad, so I removed it and put it 
into a spare machine (that didn't have a power supply). That machine 
booted normally.

I put the power supply back into the sick machine, and unplugged all the 
drives.  Same result - fans whir, no POST or HDD activity.

I removed the memory.  Same result - fans whir, no POST or HDD activity.

So now I am thinking it's the motherboard, and I order one of the only two 
mobo's Newegg offers that still supports 462(A) socket processors, the PC 
Chips M848A.  Wait for it to arrive....

It arrived yesterday. So I carefully disassembled my sick PC, put in the 
new mobo, reassembled it, turned it on... and no change. Fans spin, no 
POST, no HDD activity. 

Take out the memory, disconnect the drives, restart - same behavior.

Swap in a pouer supply from my wife's machine - same behavior.

Remove the CPU - same behavior.

So I'm at a loss for what to do next (aside from "buy a new computer").

I suppose it's possible that my old mobo is bad, AND the new one is also - 
or that the CPU has died, or that the old mobo is bad, and I damaged the 
CPU in moving it from one machine to the other.  

How can I test these various possibilities? Are there other possibilities 
that I am overlooking?

(A further note - the HD is fine; I moved the hard drive to the spare PC, 
put the power supply back in, and it booted up fine, which is how I'm 
typing this e-mail. Unfortunately, the spare machine doesn't support my 
graphics card.)

All comments welcome, thanks in advance-

--
David Fleck
david.fleck at mchsi.com

Well from what I can see, the only thing in common before and after the mobo
swap is the CPU (you already tried removing RAM).
It is possible the CPU is dead or died during swap.  However I will say my
limited previous experience with PC Chips mobos is negative, so I wouldn't
rule out both mobos being dead either.

My experience with PC Chips was with a previous job, Linux rackmount servers
reseller, we normally used a certain range of motherboard brands, but for
the entry level machines we had to switch to a new one, and chose PC Chips.
They worked for a while but tended to have a higher failure rate, or other
defects, than other low end mobos.  The higher end machines used Tyan mobos
mainly.

So I would see if you can try another mobo with your CPU (preferably another
brand), or if not, return the PC Chips to NewEgg, they have good customer
service.

Matt M.
LinuxKnight


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Sep 17 08:08:48 2008
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0809170807090.29169@grond.sourballs.org>

On Wed, 17 Sep 2008, David Fleck wrote:

> I suppose it's possible that my old mobo is bad, AND the new one is also -
> or that the CPU has died, or that the old mobo is bad, and I damaged the
> CPU in moving it from one machine to the other.

David,

   A couple of years ago I experienced my first CPU failure in 20 years.
You've replaced everything but that, so that's what I suspect as the source
of the problem.

   Can you borrow a socket-compatible CPU for testing, or bring the unit to a
store that might have such a CPU available for testing?

   If not, I'd go with a new ASUS motherboard, new CPU, and new RAM if the
old sticks won't work. After 6 years you've gotten your money's worth from
that hardware and the cost of a complete upgrade is quite likely less than
the cost of the aggrevation and trying to fix the current engine and drive
train.

Good luck,

Rich

So far, it sounds like a no video condition. my idea would be to take 
out everything, most of which you've done,  and see if it will respond 
with no daughtercards, no drives, no nothing except the video card,  
memory, cpu. see if it will come up to bios. you might check the 
monitors. will the monitor work on other systems? might be an issue.

power on for a while and see if, after warming up, it will boot.

the floppy drive and CD drive lights blink once while the mobo beeps:

I can still power down by holding the power button in for a few seconds,

I have this problem with a SATA drive from Seagate. The track 
alignment is borderline and when cold, the boot file is not found.

Seagate and there is no problem.

If you think you'll have the opportunity to enjoy more PC
troubleshooting in your future, a POST code card is a worthwhile
investment:

http://cgi.ebay.com/ws/eBayISAPI.dll?ViewItem&item=260288827933

It displays the BIOS POST codes as the machine is booting. Fancier
models (like the one above) keep a history of codes that can be
browsed later. Much easier than listening for beeps.

I'm trying to set up a redmine rails server, and I've run into a
interesting (to me) problem.

I've set up the application to use mongrel_cluster (which requires
mod_rewrite, mod_proxy, and mod_proxy_balancer).

I've had problems accessing my svn repositories once the mod_rewrite was
added in.

What I am wondering is how to tell mod_rewrite to ignore the <Location
/svn-test> and allow the http request as normal.

Thanks

NameVirtualHost *
<VirtualHost *>
	ServerAdmin webmaster at localhost
        ServerName <removed>
        DocumentRoot /var/www/rails_apps/redmine/public


  <Directory "/var/www/rails_apps/redmine/public">
    Options FollowSymLinks
    AllowOverride None
    Order allow,deny
    Allow from all
 </Directory>
  RewriteEngine On
  RewriteCond %{DOCUMENT_ROOT}/%{REQUEST_FILENAME} !-f
  RewriteRule ^/(.*)$ balancer://redmine_cluster%{REQUEST_URI} [P,QSA,L]

    <Location /svn-test>
      DAV svn
      SVNParentPath /var/lib/subversion
      #SVNPath /var/svn/repository
      AuthType Basic
      AuthName "Subversion Repository"
      AuthUserFile /etc/apache2/dav_svn.passwd
      Require valid-user
    </Location>


</VirtualHost>
<Proxy balancer://redmine_cluster>
    Order allow,deny
    Allow from all
    BalancerMember http://127.0.0.1:8000
    BalancerMember http://127.0.0.1:8001
    BalancerMember http://127.0.0.1:8002
</Proxy>

BIOS beeps:
2 short beeps: POST Error - error code shown on screen
Repeating short beeps: Power supply or system board problem

from http://www.pchell.com/hardware/beepcodes.shtml

From russell at personaltelco.net  Fri Sep 19 09:12:32 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 19 Sep 2008 09:12:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] help me understand this pin-level kernel-interface stuff
Message-ID: <86myi4dsi7.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


This is way more low-level than I normally deal with from a
programming perspective (though, yes, i can solder big things).

In what is perhaps a recap of an earlier problem I asked about here,
oh, a year or so ago (last November-ish), I would like to be able to
poll the state of an external mechanical switch (off/on) from Linux
userspace.  That is, I want to be able the read /proc/<mumble>/switch1
and get a "1" when the switch is closed or a "0" when the switch is
open.  Or something like that.

The board I am dealing with is the Alix 3D3 [1].  I inquired to PC
Engines (the designer/vendor) about what I am trying to do, and they
pointed me at a pad on the PCB labelled "S1" which was unpopulated,
but intended for a "switch", probably like a reset switch of some
kind.

Sounds like it could be perfect.  Except for:

 a) I have only the barest whiff of an idea how to get the switch
    state information from Linux user-space; and

 b) I don't know what kind of switch I should be attaching, if there
    are conventions about what else (e.g. capacitor or resistors or
    whatever) to include in the switch circuit and whether or not
    those are already on the PCB.

As for exception (a), I looked at the schematic in reference [1] and
traced the S1 pad on the board to something called <MODESW#>, which
appears to be attached to ball C9 on the CPU.  If I consult the AMD
Geode LX Processors Data Book [2], I find that ball C9 appears to be
labelled DQ29 on the Memory Data Bus.  Beyond that, my understanding
begins to degrade rather rapidly and catastrophically.  Not to be
completely thwarted, I note that there are also three LEDs that are
have an LED driver in OpenWrt's Linux kernel, so I look there [3].
Clearly, the LED state (which are also connected to DQ pins 16,
28, and 19, respectively) is writable somehow using port I/O.

I would have some chance of writing a driver to poll the state of my
switch, under the following conditions:

 A) I could locate an example of some other driver polling a similar
    state; and 

 B) I had the foggiest idea how to translate the information in [2]
    into a port number and bit offset from which to read.

Could any helpful souls out there (and yes, I am talking to you Galen,
amongst others) either solve my problem in its entirety, or barring
that, gently shove me in the direction of a solution?

Thanks!


References:

[1] http://www.pcengines.ch/schema/alix3d.pdf
[2] http://www.amd.com/files/connectivitysolutions/geode/geode_lx/33234d_lx_ds.pdf
[3] https://dev.openwrt.org/browser/trunk/target/linux/generic-2.6/files/drivers/leds/leds-alix.c

Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

> In what is perhaps a recap of an earlier problem I asked about here,
> oh, a year or so ago (last November-ish), I would like to be able to
> poll the state of an external mechanical switch (off/on) from Linux
> userspace.  That is, I want to be able the read /proc/<mumble>/switch1
> and get a "1" when the switch is closed or a "0" when the switch is
> open.  Or something like that.
>
> The board I am dealing with is the Alix 3D3 [1].  I inquired to PC
> Engines (the designer/vendor) about what I am trying to do, and they
> pointed me at a pad on the PCB labelled "S1" which was unpopulated,
> but intended for a "switch", probably like a reset switch of some
> kind.
>
> Sounds like it could be perfect.  Except for:
>
>  a) I have only the barest whiff of an idea how to get the switch
>    state information from Linux user-space; and
>
>  b) I don't know what kind of switch I should be attaching, if there
>    are conventions about what else (e.g. capacitor or resistors or
>    whatever) to include in the switch circuit and whether or not
>    those are already on the PCB.
>
> As for exception (a), I looked at the schematic in reference [1] and
> traced the S1 pad on the board to something called <MODESW#>, which
> appears to be attached to ball C9 on the CPU.  If I consult the AMD
> Geode LX Processors Data Book [2], I find that ball C9 appears to be
> labelled DQ29 on the Memory Data Bus.  Beyond that, my understanding
> begins to degrade rather rapidly and catastrophically.  Not to be

> References:
>
> [1] http://www.pcengines.ch/schema/alix3d.pdf
> [2] http://www.amd.com/files/connectivitysolutions/geode/geode_lx/33234d_lx_ds.pdf
> [3] https://dev.openwrt.org/browser/trunk/target/linux/generic-2.6/files/drivers/leds/leds-alix.c
>
Seem like you need this file:
http://www.pcengines.ch/pdf/alix2.pdf
Page 18 has the port info for the LEDs and switch.
The switch is a user level switch separate from the reset switch.

You will have to write your own code for this if the kernel you have
doesn't have drivers that provide the needed access.  The reason you
have to write this for the IO ports is the ports are 32bit and /dev/port is
8 bit operations.

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Fri Sep 19 15:59:05 2008
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Fri, 19 Sep 2008 17:59:05 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Root password for ubuntu?
In-Reply-To: <48D42D0C.80001@comcast.net>
References: <48D42D0C.80001@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4145b6790809191559g7562a44bj4d865071d3fcab16@mail.gmail.com>

Yes.  Ubuntu makes use of sudo.  So, whenever the update manager asks
for a password, you should enter your own password, assuming you are
an admin on the machine.

If you do ever want to become root at the command line, just type "sudo su -"

Regards,
- Robert

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Sep 19 19:19:08 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 19 Sep 2008 19:19:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Looking for suggestions / first-hand experience with
 tablet PCs.
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80809191119k78843a55m4f5460272e271a72@mail.gmail.com>
References: <c834ec80809191119k78843a55m4f5460272e271a72@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080919191908.5d66431a.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Fri, 19 Sep 2008 11:19:12 -0700
"Rogan Creswick" <creswick at gmail.com> dijo:

> I'm seriously considering a Tablet PC for my next machine (to replace
> the venerable T42p) and I'm defaulting to the Lenovo X61 tablet --
> I"ve had fantastic experiences with my T42, and it seems like the
> reasonable choice.  Before biting the bullet, however, I'd like to see
> if anyone else has experience with tablets (running Linux, of course,
> I very seriously could not care less how well they work under
> windows).
> 
> My biggest hardware concerns are: screen resolution and battery life
> (1280x1024 is *really* pushing what I can stand) and the ability to
> drive suitably large displays when docked / at home.  (Where "suitably
> large" would ideally be dual 1600x1200's, but could be as little as a
> single 24" wide-screen.)  Anything less than 4-5 hours of battery life
> is also probably a deal-breaker.
> 
> I'm also somewhat concerned about the software support for the
> pen-based UI, and whether it will really be a viable means of input
> when writing / coding on a plane.

I have never used a tablet PC, but I do have some experience with a
Lenovo T61. Of all that you mention above, battery life is something I
would check out thoroughly. Lenovo claims 4-5 hours battery life for my
machine, but the best I can get is about two and a half. Yet I know
people running Windows on the same machine who get the rated battery
life. The majority of the problem is power management in Linux, which
evidently is not as smart as the Windows version. Do not ask me to
define "smart," as I have on a very dim understanding of how power
management works. There are also issues involving which kind of
graphics card your machine has. 

I strongly recommend the Thinkpad e-list:

The linux-thinkpad mailing list home page is at:
http://mailman.linux-thinkpad.org/mailman/listinfo/linux-thinkpad

I bet someone there will be able to answer your questions.

John Jason Jordan (johnxj at comcast.net) typed this ...
> I have never used a tablet PC, but I do have some experience with a
> Lenovo T61. Of all that you mention above, battery life is something I
> would check out thoroughly. Lenovo claims 4-5 hours battery life for my

I also have a T61, but I have the integrated video (Intel, not NVidia),
and I get 4.5-5 hours on battery. This machine is maxed out with 4G RAM,
15.4" widescreen, 7200rpm 200G SATA2 drive, etc. 

So the rated battery life isn't a complete lie, but some options will
certainly prevent you from getting it.

I'm fairly comfortable with the advertised battery life ratings, at
least on thinkpads.  My T42 gets 4-5 hours on a fresh (and new) 9-cell
battery, and it has the most power-hungry video configuration I could
find for it.  (although I often turn down the screen brightness and
disable wifi if I really need the longevity).

My concerns about battery life are more to filter out suggestions of a
no-name tablet that gets an advertised 2-3 hours on a charge.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Sep 19 20:33:06 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 19 Sep 2008 20:33:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Adding Partitions to fstab in Ubuntu
Message-ID: <48D46EF2.7030901@comcast.net>

Just when you think you learned how to do something, they change things. 
(Well, they probably changed things a long time ago, but I'm just 
finding out!)

I saved a copy of fstab from my old install of SuSE 10.1. I figured I'd 
just copy parts of it for the new install. SuSE 10.1 used the old 
familiar hda1, hda2, hdb1, hdb2, etc. Ubuntu uses sda1, etc. So, off to 
the documentation to see what to do. There I learn that Ubuntu now 
recommends UUIDs instead of specific device names. The docs provided 
details on how to compose the lines to be added to fstab. There must be 
something wrong with the way I followed the instructions because when I 
restart the system it hangs. I was able to rename the backup copy of 
fstab and finish rebooting.

What I'm trying to do is mount the four partitions of my second hard 
drive at mount-points in my home directory, /home/rsteff. I followed the 
directions to list the drives by UUID:

rsteff at moonguide:~$ ls -l /dev/disk/by-uuid/
total 0
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
2b235423-d1fe-47c9-afbb-f7bd1a23d686 -> ../../sda3
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
5ae6428e-d0ab-4f71-8266-11e04a0cb070 -> ../../sdb3
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
7d18641f-4ba0-4944-bf78-3df10b11b412 -> ../../sda1
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
802b6c08-f4c4-443a-9f22-5104642b543a -> ../../sdb4
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
9615a8e5-76f8-4368-b745-b730df45c437 -> ../../sdb2
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
e0410871-bb9b-4967-aa7c-4063a423c153 -> ../../sdb1
lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 10 2008-09-19 18:09 
e0977a64-ba0a-4773-92b7-6d78f34be244 ->

All the partitions have ext3 file systems on them, and I can mount and 
read them with the Gnome gui tools.

I didn't see anything in the documentation about "realtime", but I 
guessed -- perhaps incorrectly -- that since that's what the other two 
partitions use, it should work for these four. Likewise for the entries 
for <dump> and <pass>.

Here is my fstab with the errant lines:

-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
# /etc/fstab: static file system information.
#
# <file system> <mount point>   <type>  <options>       <dump>  <pass>
proc            /proc           proc    defaults        0       0
# /dev/sda2
UUID=e0977a64-ba0a-4773-92b7-6d78f34be244 /               ext3    
relatime,errors=remount-ro 0       1
# /dev/sda3
UUID=2b235423-d1fe-47c9-afbb-f7bd1a23d686 /home           ext3    
relatime        0       2
# /dev/sda1
UUID=7d18641f-4ba0-4944-bf78-3df10b11b412 none            swap    
sw              0       0
/dev/scd0       /media/cdrom0   udf,iso9660 user,noauto,exec,utf8 0       0
/dev/fd0        /media/floppy0  auto    rw,user,noauto,exec,utf8 0       0
# /dev/sdb1
UUID=e0410871-bb9b-4967-aa7c-4063a423c153 /home/music    ext3    
realtime    0    2
# /dev/sdb2
UUID=9615a8e5-76f8-4368-b745-b730df45c437 /home/photos    ext3    
realtime    0    2
# /dev/sdb3
UUID=7d18641f-4ba0-4944-bf78-3df10b11b412 /home/backups    ext3    
realtime    0    2
# /dev/sdb4
UUID=9615a8e5-76f8-4368-b745-b730df45c437 /home/spare    ext3    
realtime    0    2
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

So, where did I go wrong?

(BTW, I did have success with the rest of the install, even moving my 
T-bird profile back onto the desktop, from which I am sending this e-mail.)

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Sat Sep 20 08:15:02 2008
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Sat, 20 Sep 2008 10:15:02 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Adding Partitions to fstab in Ubuntu
In-Reply-To: <48D46EF2.7030901@comcast.net>
References: <48D46EF2.7030901@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4145b6790809200815ifd1cadeh9615adc0894e3768@mail.gmail.com>

FWIW, you can still do things the "old way".   For example, this works
on my system:

/dev/sda1   /   ext3   errors=remount-ro   0   1

as does the UUID way:

UUID=538f72a2-fbc0-454f-a058-32d1a0cae8b3   /   ext3   errors=remount-ro   0   1

as does the LABEL way:

LABEL=ubuntu   /   ext3   errors=remount-ro   0   1

They all work.  So if you are more comfortable with the "old way" feel
free to use that.

As for relatime, I have no idea when or why it would be used.  Seems
to be important if you use mutt (from the man page):

relatime
  Update inode access times relative to modify or change time.  Access
time is only updated if the previous access  time  was  earlier than
the  current  modify  or change time. (Similar to noatime, but doesn't
break mutt or other applications that need to know if a file has been
read since the last time it was modified.)

Regards,
- Robert

relatime
Update inode access times relative to modify or change
time. Access time is only updated if the previous access
time was earlier than the current modify or change time.
(Similar to noatime, but doesn?t break mutt or other
applications that need to know if a file has been read
since the last time it was modified.)

One of the drawbacks to learning by copying what someone else did, and 
also being impatient, is not taking the time to fully understand 
everything.

is the same error. I paid more attention this time as saw that it save 
the relevant lines in a log file:

On Sat, 20 Sep 2008 06:29:03 -0700 (PDT)
Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> dijo:

> On Fri, 19 Sep 2008, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> 
> > The majority of the problem is power management in Linux, which evidently
> > is not as smart as the Windows version.
> 
>    Interesting comment. On all the portables I've owned (Toshibas, IBMs,
> Sony) the battery life I get with my linux installations is about twice that
> reported by the manufacturer with the default Winduhs installation.

I should have added that my T61 has the nVidia Quadro 140M. As I have
read often on the Thinkpad e-list, nVidia graphics will suck a lot more
current than the Intel or ATI options. 

I should also have added that I currently use Hardy x86_64, upgraded
from Gutsy. When I had Gutsy the battery life was an hour and a half.
One thing that happened after the upgrade to Hardy was that the screen
went dim after a few seconds of non-use while on battery. That never
happened with Gutsy. I think there were some other enhancements to
power management in Hardy, but I don't recall all of them. I think this
affected only the x86_64 versions - 32-bit users had better power
management from the beginning.

Also, when I am on battery it is because I am away from home, which is
wired for ethernet. I don't even use wireless at home. But when away
from home I am using wireless constantly, and that is a known power
sucker.

Fortunately for me, the two and a half hours I get now is adequate.
There are no classes that last more than two hours, and if I am in the
library there are always places available with an outlet for the brick. 

It is interesting to note the wide variety of experiences that people
here have with laptop battery life. There are a great many variables.

You may find the powertop tool to be interesting to run.  Many people
don't realize how much impact userspace applications have on power
consumption, especially when they aren't "compute intensive" apps, and
some userspace apps are just stupid about performance/power issues.

http://www.lesswatts.org/projects/powertop/

Before I tried the old way, I decided to take one more close look at 
exactly what I typed. Turns out the problem was paying attention to 
details. I had pasted an invalid UUID number for one of the partitions 
(no idea where I got it), and I had left out my home directory name in 
the mount points -- i.e. I had /home/music instead of 
/home/rsteff/music. This works in my fstab:

---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
# /dev/sdb1

ext3    relatime    0    2
#
# /dev/sdb4
UUID=802b6c08-f4c4-443a-9f22-5104642b543a /home/rsteff/spare    ext3    
relatime    0    2
---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

All four directories are where I expect them to be.

If the wifi chip is Broadcom, depending on which one it is, you should be
able to use it still in Linux, either with the ndiswrapper (which uses the
Windoze driver), or with fwcutter, which uses a non-free (as in license not
price) firmware loader blob.  Most PPC laptop Macs have Broadcom wifi
chipset, and for ex. on my G4 PowerBook, after installing Ubuntu, using
their restricted drivers tool, it installed fwcutter and the necessary
Broadcom firmware, and it works like a champ.

Next time you are looking for a Linux compatible laptop, check places like
www.linux-laptop.net, for reviews of other Linux users.  Not sure how many
NewEgg reviewers run Linux... ;)
NewEgg is good stuff, no doubt, my current desktop has upgraded innards all
from NewEgg, just have to look elsewhere for Linux compatibility.
There is no review yet for this Acer on linux-laptop.net.... perhaps once
you get it up and running you should post about it on a blog or something
and submit it to them :).


PS- I'll see you at Lindependence Day (and our planning meeting), probably
with a laptop running Fedora.

-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

> > http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.aspx?Item=N82E16834115497
> >
> > Someone mentioned that it has a Broadcom chipset that's not so Linux
> > friendly, but I didn't see that listed in the specs. It has a AMD M690G
> > chipset.
> >
> > Has anyone had experience with one of these or other Acer laptops? The
> > reason I got this is to promote Linux by sharing the hdd with Vista to
> show
> > people how well Linux works. I'm doing a Linux workshop Oct. 25 and
> wanted
> > to show it off there. I plan to install Linux Mint, PCLOS or openSUSE on
> > it.

> > David
> > _______________________________________________
> > PLUG mailing list
> > PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> > http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
> >
>
> If the wifi chip is Broadcom, depending on which one it is, you should be
> able to use it still in Linux, either with the ndiswrapper (which uses the
> Windoze driver), or with fwcutter, which uses a non-free (as in license not
> price) firmware loader blob.  Most PPC laptop Macs have Broadcom wifi
> chipset, and for ex. on my G4 PowerBook, after installing Ubuntu, using
> their restricted drivers tool, it installed fwcutter and the necessary
> Broadcom firmware, and it works like a champ.
>
> Next time you are looking for a Linux compatible laptop, check places like
> www.linux-laptop.net, for reviews of other Linux users.  Not sure how many
> NewEgg reviewers run Linux... ;)
> NewEgg is good stuff, no doubt, my current desktop has upgraded innards all
> from NewEgg, just have to look elsewhere for Linux compatibility.
> There is no review yet for this Acer on linux-laptop.net.... perhaps once
> you get it up and running you should post about it on a blog or something
> and submit it to them :).
>
>
> PS- I'll see you at Lindependence Day (and our planning meeting), probably
> with a laptop running Fedora.
>
> --
> ----------
> Matt M.
> LinuxKnight
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

From mike at mommabears.com  Mon Sep 22 12:13:46 2008
From: mike at mommabears.com (MJang)
Date: Mon, 22 Sep 2008 12:13:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu GDM won't load
In-Reply-To: <48D7DA0D.8050103@comcast.net>

Message-ID: <1222110826.7043.29.camel@UbuntuHH>

On Mon, 2008-09-22 at 10:46 -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
>  * Running local boot scripts (/etc/rc.local.)                    [OK]
> _ (cursor)

To confirm, the system stops when - or just after the /etc/rc.local
script. If I'm hearing you right, this is a new installation, with
nothing in the /etc/rc.local script which could stop the install. 

S99rc.local is a script in the /etc/rc2.d/ directory. That means all
other Start scripts (which start with an S) with numbers less than 99
have run OK. I think two other scripts run in this directory just after
- (if I'm assuming right, that scripts are run in alphabetical order).

S99rmnologin
S99stop-readahead 

Since the rmnologin script does start the display manager, that seems to
confirm your suspicion of a problem with GDM.

In any case, if there are problems with the noted scripts, you should be
able to boot into recovery mode. It brings up a mostly blue screen,
where you can select an option like "drop to root shell prompt" 

where you have administrative access - and should also have networking.
At that point you'll be able to copy the files you want for the
"syncrhonization" you've described.

Thanks,
Mike



From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Sep 22 12:47:04 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 22 Sep 2008 12:47:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Received Mail difficult to forward
In-Reply-To: <48D7DC16.6050102@dslnorthwest.net>
References: <48D7DC16.6050102@dslnorthwest.net>
Message-ID: <20080922194704.GC19436@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Sep 22, 2008 at 10:55:34AM -0700, Denis Heidtmann wrote:
> I have received a few emails which contain text and pictures. These have 
> come forwarded to me from a couple of different people.  When I try to 
> forward the email the pictures are missing ...
...
> Can anybody tell me what is happening?  Is there a way for me to change the 
> way TB handles these so that I can forward them without having to painfully 
> reconstruct them by saving each image separately and attaching the saved 
> images?

I don't know what it is called in Thunderbird, but with the text-
based Mutt mailer this is called a "bounce" instead of the normal
"forward".  Bounce sends a duplicate of the message, attachments
and all, to the email address specified in the bounce command.
Hopefully, TB has something similar, and perhaps the name will
help you find it.

Keith

> 
I find "bounce e-mail" or "bounce mail" to mean returning to the sender:
DEFINITION - Bounce e-mail (sometimes referred to as bounce mail) is electronic 
mail that is returned to the sender because it cannot be delivered for some reason.
Except: Some products and users use the term bounce to mean "forward a received 
note to someone else."

That in fact is what I tried to do.  Usually, I can forward a message having 
attachments, and the attachments appear in the copy.  Same with "edit as new": 
the attachments are attached to the copy which I am editing.

However, TB shows the troublesome mail as having no attachments.

Investigating more, I saved the email as a file (A file menu option in TB).  I 
then opened the resulting saved file using Kmail.  It shows it as an HTML 
message having 24 attachments (the images).  It lists various message part types 
as multipart/alternative, Plain Text Document, multipart/related, HTML Document, 
and JPEG Images. (I have never used Kmail before. but it tells me much more 
about this message than TB does.)

It seems at this point that this is a TB issue with this type of message. My 
guess at present is that the problem is that it is an HTML message.  I searched 
a little for another HTML message, but the one I found did not have attachments, 
so I cannot determine if all HTML messages suffer the same fate.  Time to Google 
some, now that I have a suspicion of what to look for.

-Denis

According to dnsreport.com, the only questionable part of your setup is your 
that your mailserver returns "server2.frankhunt.com" but the A record 
is "frankhunt.com", which is a violation of RFC821. It's not likely that 
causes you to lose email, though.

Have you checked your Postfix logs?

-Aaron

Start with your mail server logs.  Typically /var/log/maillog.  See if 
the connections are even coming in.

I just saw Aaron's post and I'll say I've had many problems with Yahoo. 
  Some of those directly related to DNS issues.  Each mail server Yahoo 
uses seems to make up it's own mind on what to do with questionable DNS 
entries.  Get this fixed and as those changes propagate look at the logs.

I just tried a telnet to your server and it responds quickly.  But that 
made me think of a problem an ISP I worked for had with Yahoo and its 
groups.  User Quality.  The typical postfix install has a header_checks 
file that catches Yahoo Groups all the time.  Six (6) of the same 
character in a row.  Your logs should show this as 'ACL 3' message.


Rod

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Sep 23 13:56:49 2008

> the behavior I want. Could someone point me in the right direction?

Rich,

I'm using Alpine 2.00, so the menu options may be different than they 
are in the Pine 4.64 you're using. In Alpine, there's a section under 
Rules -> Roles called CURRENT MESSAGE CONDITIONS BEGIN HERE. In that 
section, you can set the To: address that triggers a role change.

The change ends up reflected in the "patterns-roles" directive in 
.pinerc.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From jhoblitt at ifa.hawaii.edu  Tue Sep 23 13:48:27 2008
From: jhoblitt at ifa.hawaii.edu (Joshua Hoblitt)
Date: Tue, 23 Sep 2008 10:48:27 -1000
Subject: [PLUG] Creative SB X-Fi issues
In-Reply-To: <670984.93694.qm@web50604.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
References: <670984.93694.qm@web50604.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20080923204827.GD28007@ifa.hawaii.edu>

Robert,

In general (but not all cases) an OS running under VMWare does not talk to the
actual hardware.  VMWare emulates "virtual hardware" that will work with, often
a very simple, driver under the OS that is being hosted.  Thus in your case you
get sound with Linux under VMware as Linux is using a very
simple/common/generic driver which is talking to the fake VMware hardware which
is in turn talking to the "real" Win32 X-FI driver.

-J
